This specific issue covers the nations of Thedas, such as what every culture wears, the classes, how to pronounce some of each nation's dialect and language, species, and events that have happened since humans first arrived on the continent. (Elves and Dwarves go back much further, and some information is told about life before humans as well, although that time is \\"less documented\\".) There's also a timeline of events in Thedas all through out the book - very useful when you've read the books, comics, and played all the games, to then fully grasp the structure of when each event occurred. B009NY43NY|3.0|For me, books and movies are alike in one thing: it's all about the ending. It truly won't matter if the beginning was sluggish, or if the book or movie didn't make any sense, as long as it has a satisfying ending. That just doesn't happen here.<br /><br />Let me start from the beginning.<br /><br />The Sookie Stackhouse novels are, in my opinion, much better than the television show. In fact, I see them as two entirely different things, with BookSookie being much more relatable, beautiful and strong than TrueBloodSookie. That being said, it is understandable why so many people fell in love with her and why so many of us craved a well deserved Happily Ever After for our heroine. Unfortunately for us, somewhere in the midst of the glitz and glamour of Hollywood, Charlaine Harris lost her way and she wound up writing Dead Ever After.<br /><br />What can I say about this book? Here comes spoilers!<br /><br />The Good:<br /><br />1. We see a lot of characters making a comeback as a final farewell to all of us. Quinn, Barry, Amelia, Bob, Claude, Johan Glassport, Steve Newlin, Desmond Cataliades, a mention of Hunter, Remy and Niall, Sam's mother, Arlene (of all people) and Copley Carmichael, among others.<br /><br />2. I always enjoy spending a day with Sookie, so I seriously didn't mind going shopping with her, cooking with her and gardening with her. If you truly care for this character you will enjoy what she does, and she enjoys doing these things. They come as a nice distraction from the many terrible things happening in her life.<br /><br />3. Sam and Sookie sitting in a tree... COME ON! You had to see this coming since Book One! If you didn't, you just weren't paying attenion. Sam was always there for Sookie. Sookie always counted on Sam, watched out for him, gave him a loan of her money... Crap... She even gave up the cluviel dor for the man! I agree that after all of the passionate sex scenes with luscious Eric, Sam and Sookie seem very tame and underworked. Harris could have spent a bit more time creating sexual and romantic tension between these two, but I overlook that because, like I said, I expected it to happen since the beginning.<br /><br />The Bad:<br /><br />1. The murder plot was sloppy at best, exasperating at its worst. It seemed like Harris really didn't know how to end Sookie's story so she decided to bring out every single character that had a personal vendetta against poor Sookie and have them all try to kill her, or torture her, at once. Like I said: sloppy.<br /><br />2. Eric. We all knew this was coming. She had been working this for awhile now to a point in which I really felt indifferent towards his relationship with Sookie. Still... I liked Eric. I liked that he knew what he wanted and was strong enough to go after it. I liked that he always tried, in his own Eric Northman way, to take care of Sookie and I liked that he cared, in his own inimitable way, about his children. I really think that he deserved a better farewell and it made me feel like Harris really dislikes the character she worked so hard to make us love.<br /><br />3. Sam and Sookie. YES, I did say it was one of the \\"Good\\" parts, but the way it was rushed, the way the sex scene was laughable and the way they treated one another (like strangers) was just wrong! I wanted them to end up together, but I would have written it better. Much better.<br /><br />The Middle Ground:<br /><br />1. There have been a lot of complaints about how Sookie will relate to vampires from now on. Some people remind us that Harris has likened the Vampire and Were cause to the LGBT community and it seems wrong to them the way Sookie's relationship with Vamps end. It doesn't seem wrong to me at all. Just because you can relate to a cause, be it Vampires, Werewolves or LGBT doesn't mean that you have to become one of them or live with them. You can back them up, sympathize with them and be their friend and nothing more. What most people seem to forget is that Sookie has been beaten, raped and traumatized by her relationship with vampires. I won't hold it against Harris that she chooses for Sookie to not want to relate to them intimately anymore. I find it strange that some people do.<br /><br />It all winds out to the ending. Was it good? Was it bad? The jury's still out on that one. I wanted more. I wanted more passion. I wanted more happiness for Sookie and Sam. I wanted Bill to go on and be happy...<br /><br />I wanted more. You will want more too. But maybe that is the whole point of this book: life and love go on. And for Sookie this isn't the end. It's the beginning of the rest of her life.

B00BSWMITK|3.0|Ok...Unstoppable Momentum....<br /><br />As with any Joe Satriani album, I have to process it over several listens. My current impression is like with \\"Strange Beautiful Music\\" and \\"Is There Love in Space\\".<br /><br />There are moments of shear compositional and technical brilliance here and there and then moments where you are scratching your head saying to yourself...\\"Man, what was Satch thinking when he laid that down?\\". I feel obligated to buy it just because I really want to support him, but several of the tracks are so boring and 'inaccessible', it just seem like filler. A couple of the tracks though are so groovy and infectious you just can't help but bob your head right along with the tune.<br /><br />The first two tracks, \\"Unstoppable Momentum\\", \\"Can't go Back\\", and the track \\"A Door into Summer\\" are all fantastic. \\"Jumpin' In\\" and \\"Jumpin' Out\\" are both funky hillbilly vibe tunes, maybe reminiscent of his \\"Banana Mango\\" phase, but the solo sections in both of these tracks are off the hook awesome, especially on \\"Jumpin' Out\\"! Whenever Satch explores those minor lines, his tunes just have an incredible gravity to them and reminds me why he's one of my favorite guitar players.<br /><br />\\"Weight of the World\\" begins with a \\"Bamboo\\"esque intro and then turns into a dance track with a rather depressing melody and it just doesn't work very well. Certainly one of those \\"scratch yer head\\" tracks. I was toying with Joe's opening to his track \\"Time Machine\\", using that open D and then fretting notes on the G and B to make little melodies when Ifirst started playing guitar. To have Joe construct a whole song around that is just amateurish at best. No progression, just a locked in, staccato rythym. It reminds me of his track \\"The Golden Room\\" which I call \\"The Song that Never Went Anywhere.\\" Its not that its bad...its that it just leaves you disappointed, unfulfilled and frustrated because you know Joe is capable of writing, composing, and playing so much better than this.<br /><br />Also, their isn't a ballad at all on the disk.  So disappointing.<br /><br />Anyway...those are my initial impressions. I'll have to digest it more...maybe it will grow on me more over time. But when you've been listening to Jack Thammarat (Thailand), Marco Sfogli (Italy), and Rick Graham (UK) for the last few months on YouTube (whom I now pretty much consider to be \\"The Second Coming of Instrumental Rock Guitar\\") this offering from Joe just doesn't measure up very well.<br /><br />Just IMHO...<br /><br />**Peace**
B008AUGEFC|5.0|I've only read two of David Mitchell's novels (this and [[ASIN:0812974018 Black Swan Green: A Novel]]) but it's enough to tell me this is a man who has great ambition and talent.  I don't know about other readers, but I often find myself being intimidated by authors whose books win awards like this one (it won several, including the British Book Awards, and was shortlisted for end-of-the-year accolades like the Booker Prize, the Nebula, and the Arthur C. Clarke Award).  But rest assured, Mitchell's work is as reader friendly as it is impressive.<br /><br />In that vein, some friends I've described his books to have made comments like, \\"six stories within one novel - and they're not even in the same time period, or share narrators?  No way.\\" Again, while Mitchell may sound intimidating, ATLAS is thoroughly enjoyable, very readable, and actually fun at times (the fourth story's a hoot!). The six stories within actually link up in unusual and clever ways, and I did not find this novel hard to follow.  I'll relate the bare bones of the first few stories to point out how interesting they are.<br /><br />The first story - \\"The Pacific Journal of Adam Ewing\\" - begins and ends the novel. As it relates the story of a young notary whose business takes him to the South Pacific in the mid-1850s, he is horrified by their society's treatment of slaves, and aghast when he finds out one has smuggled himself inside his berth on his return home.  But Ewing's health becomes a big issue on the long voyage home...so much so that I was shocked when it just seemed to end in the middle of a sentence. (Don't worry...it concludes at the end of the book. )<br /><br />The next tale moved ahead 80 years to the 1930s: \\"Letters from Zedelghem\\" concerns a young, bankrupt con artist named Robert Frobisher whose letters to his male lover Rufus Sixsmith inform us how he is exploiting his considerable talent to secure a place at the side of a languishing Belgian composer who seems unable to finish his masterwork. Soon, Robert begins frequenting the bedroom of the composer's wife, and in his spare time he writes his own musical composition and lounges within the composer's estate reading the first half of a journal of a young man named...Adam Ewing.<br /><br />Next up is \\"Half Lives: The First Luisa Rey Mystery\\" and find ourselves in the 1970s, within the mind of a resourceful young female reporter who is being handed a bombshell of a story: an elderly scientist named Rufus Sixsmith has evidence that a nuclear power plant about to start operating is highly dangerous. He is murdered soon after, and her investigation turns up not only his report, but also his letters from his old male lover...Robert Frobisher. As she is assembling her story she ends up buying Frobisher's magnum opus: \\"The Cloud Atlas Sextet.\\" But her story takes an abrupt pause after a malevolent pursuer forces her VW off a bridge - plunging her into the sea below.<br /><br />Thus it continues: the next story, \\"the Ghastly Ordeal of Timothy Cavendish,\\" is actually uproarious, as its overweight protagonist is a horrid but hilarious modern day book publisher who finds himself imprisoned in an old age home. \\"An Orison of Sonmi 451,\\" the fifth story, was the slowest for me, as it was constructed as a futuristic interview of a genetically engineered clone created merely to serve food at a restaurant. When she mysteriously becomes sentient, however, she becomes the target of several groups who wish to use her to further their own agendas. What happens to her becomes somewhat clearer when the sixth and final and only continuously told story occurs: \\"Sloosha's Crossin' an' Ev'rythin' After\\"<br /><br />It was the sixth story, occurring at an unnamed time in the post-apocalyptic future, that I found most fascinating, and it captivated me from the very first words: \\"Old \\"Georgie's path an' mine crossed more times'n I'm comfy mem'ryin, an' after I'm died, no sayin' what that fangy devil won't try an' do to me...\\" I included this quote because, while I thought this sing-song dialect rolled off the literary ear as smoothly as silk, there are many who HATE dialect in print. Their minds reel at memories of Huck Finn's Jim, or of certain passages in Faulkner, or any other stories in which authors take liberties with the printed word. But for me it worked, and I loved reading this section, for many reasons: we learn of the near end of civilization, of the savagery exhibited by the tribal clusters still left alive, and of man's hope of living on. Mostly, however, I found this story to be VERY compelling...I loved the characters and rooted for them more than any other story.<br /><br />It is at this point that the book \\"devolves,\\" returning to each tale in reverse order to wrap up their portions of the book. As this happens the reader is able to see the interlocking elements of life...how one human can affect another, even hundreds, thousands, or more. Mitchell's unusual novel is a puzzle, but it is not immensely difficult. It is a story of interconnectedness, a reaffirming collection of individual stories that shows us we are NOT merely solitary people, but members of a common humanity, one related to all.<br /><br />Mitchell's CLOUD ATLAS is after all, then, a clever story but not TOO clever, a literary puzzle that is very solvable, and a tale of separate stories that is really quite unified in its overall approach.  Mitchell is fast becoming one of my favorite authors, and I look forward to reading [[ASIN:0375507264 Number9Dream]] next.<br /><br />(A NOTE: if you give a negative review, please leave a comment to help me do better next time.)..\\" I included this quote because, while I thought this sing-song dialect rolled off the literary ear as smoothly as silk, there are many who HATE dialect in print. Give the fans what they want! I understand that books move at a slower pace and are able to be SO much more detailed when it comes to character/plot development. However, I have seen some pretty good film adaptations that have satisfied fans in the 2 hours allotted.<br /><br />\\"Beautiful Creatures\\" Pros:<br /><br />- Great cast. I thought they did a good job casting the characters. I especially thought that Alden (who plays Ethan Wate) did a phenomenal job, especially for coming into the flim last minute. He was pretty much how I pictured his character to be. Alice (who plays Lena Duchanne) grew on me as the film continued.<br /><br />- Charming set. I felt like the set captured the feel of the books - no complaints<br /><br />\\"Beautiful Creatures\\" Cons:<br /><br />WHERE DO I BEGIN?!<br />- Mythology poorly set up. \\"Beautiful Creatures\\" is the first book in a series of 3. Therefore, it sets up the mythology for an entire series. The beginning of the movie does not set up the correct mythology, and I felt confused. The book addresses the concept of destiny and how much control we have re: our destinies. Lena and Ethan are supposed to have a deep connection - where they share dreams, speak telepathically, etc. The book begins with Ethan dreaming about this girl - who he finds out later is Lena - and he hears this hypnotic song called \\"Sixteen Moons.\\" It is clear that there is a strong connection between them, even before they meet.  I felt like there was no connection between the two of them in the movie whatsoever.<br /><br />- Pace too fast. I felt like she was a pretty important character and I was irritated that she was removed. Amma is the glue in the family...we don't really see any of this! Her death causes Ethan's father to go into a deep depression and lock himself into his office saying that he is writing a novel. The pieces that Ethan's mom left behind are important to discovering what the connection between Gatlin/Casters/Lena/Ethan and I feel like if those points were addressed the story would have made some more sense. It is also insinuated in the book that Ethan's mom may not have actually died of a car crash, but rather her death is the result of Caster involvement. 1554685133|5.0|This book reminded me, as it did another reviewer, of the seventeenth-century picaresque novels in Spain. But whereas those novels portrayed usually self-serving, impoverished young men forced by their miserable circumstances into devious strategies to come out on  top, with mixed success, this novel creates a believable, thoroughly likeable boy, whose name was bestowed on him, a foundling baby, to give him a place in all three major Indian cultures, Ram Mohammed Thomas.  The structure of the book is clever, each chapter to explain the chapter in his life which prepared him to correctly answer a question in a quiz show game. A wide variety of characters and social situations people its pages, allowing insight into many aspects of Indian life. The home which bought boys and deliberately crippled them to send them out to beg and bring back their earnings was both believable and appalling, as was the chapter about a gang of thieves which got on a train and robbed all the travellers, taking all of Ram's hard-earned savings from a job working for an Australian diplomatic family. And many more, offering a panoply of detailed vignettes on a tough life, with characters who still found the resources to make lifelong friendships, enrich their lives by watching Bollywood movies many times each, and helping and defending those in need of their help, including an autistic boy who helps Ram and is helped by him in turn. Ram's success at becoming a tour guide at the Taj Mahal is funny and inspiring, as is his falling in love with a young prostitute, born to a tribe which routinely makes one daughter in each family become a prostitute.<br /><br />Tongue in cheek and sly in its allusions and inventiveness, this is a book which I enjoyed immensely, and I hope that the author will write more like it!

0812983459|5.0|The Man Booker prize badly needs a refreshingly uplifting novel like Rachel Joyce's \\"The Unlikely Pilgrimage of Harold Fry\\" to rid the reading public of its perception that the prize attracts only the most obscure, elitist and inaccessible titles recommended by publishing houses each year. Pity it made last year's longlist but went no further.<br /><br />The story of Harold Fry does not need recounting. What he did one morning after receiving a short letter from his one time colleague of years long past seems absurd on the surface but is anything but. It is as much an act of will as it is one of faith and courage that every man who has lost his way must muster within himself if he is to regain his path back to humanity. It may be the single most decisive thing Harold has ever done in his life and it's important that he makes the finishing line, regardless of the outcome. The journey is as, if not more, important than the destination. It is where Harold relearns what it means to be human.<br /><br />It's when his sojourn on foot becomes the subject of media attention and he gets waylaid and distracted by all and sundry along the way that he gets tired, confused and frustrated. He loses the purity of purpose amidst the noise around him. That's when the novel also begins to stall and lose some of its emotional resonance. Is the author sending us a message ? I'm not sure though I'd like to think so.<br /><br />This book has all the best qualities of literary fiction and a special charm that cannot fail to register with readers. Don't miss it.

B008DK3PJC|5.0|(Note: This review is based on the 1999 release of this material ([[ASIN:B000026D4J Beethoven: The Complete String Quartets]]). Based on the back cover of this box there is no difference in contents from the previous release, besides the packaging. I have upgraded my rating to reflect the excellence of the performances.)<br /><br />(4.5 of 5 stars)<br /><br />It may not bear mentioning for most people on this page, but these works are considered to be at the heart of the string quartet repertoire, and chamber music repertoire in general. Personally speaking, the late quartets in particular are some of my favorite compositions in all of music. They offer a singular perspective on the final musical thoughts of Ludwig van Beethoven's life, and seem to transcend time and genre. The quartets as a whole are notable for offering a fairly complete cross-section of Beethoven's progress as a composer while revealing some of his most experimental and personal musical expressions.<br /><br />Performances: The Alban Berg Quartett approach these work with a Viennese grace that acknowledges the classical roots of Beethoven's music while highlighting the energetic and frequently unsettled nature that define his idiom. In taking this balanced approach they can claim equal success in such distinct movements as the lengthy andante of Op. 131 and the off-kilter vivace of Op. 135. Technically their playing is impeccable, with excellent intonation and disciplined ensemble.<br /><br />I find myself most drawn to the ABQ's accounts of the early and late quartets. Their middle quartets are very good as well, but for some reason don't quite stand alongside my favorites (see the Takacs, Emerson, Cleveland quartets).<br /><br />Sound Quality: Sound seems to be a sticking point for this set, which was recorded between 1978 and 1983 - the middle and early groups first (in analogue) and finally the late quartets (in early digital). The early and middle groups were then digitally remastered in 1985. The sound overall is spacious, in the same vein as the Takacs cycle, but with just a bit less clarity of texture - unsurprising given the two decades separating the two cycles. Also, I find that the upper registers (violins especially) can be overrepresented and excessively bright at times, and the sound can get rough at the loudest dynamics. To my ears the late quartets benefit slightly from their later recording date, being a bit clearer and better balanced. In my experience these recordings seem particularly sensitive to speaker/headphone quality - I am much more satisfied with the sound when using my best headphones. Finally, if you listen to MP3s it is a good idea to use a high bit rate when you rip the music. Overall I prefer the sound of more recent recordings (like Takacs, Cleveland, and ESQ) but I still find these recordings quite listenable.<br /><br />CD Issues: The most irritating things about this set have nothing to do with the recording, but rather with the CDs. First, there are four tracks in the middle quartets in which two movements have been combined into a single track. This was a deliberate decision, as it is reflected in the track list in the booklet, but I can't think of a good rationale for doing this. Also, the order of the quartets may irk those who seek to easily locate a particular quartet. Early, middle and late quartets are jumbled together on each disc, perhaps in an effort to provide a program-like experience. In reality, I don't tend to listen to more than one quartet at a time and so flipping through the discs to find the one I want is an irritation. If you listen primarily to MP3s these problems are easily dealt with using basic software by creating playlists or changing the metadata, and by separating out the combined movements (this can all be done in iTunes). Then, if you plan on listening to CDs, you could burn the quartets in the desired order. For MP3 listeners proficient in iTunes or similar software these quirks will bea minor initial inconvenience, but for others it could present an insurmountable obstacle to enjoying the music.<br /><br />Price: The last thing to consider about this set is its price, which makes it one of the most inexpensive complete sets of Beethoven's quartets.<br /><br />Conclusion: If you can come to terms with the obnoxious CD issues and find the sound quality acceptable, the Alban Berg Quartett's Beethoven cycle is a great deal. Frankly, because there are some great modern recordings with better balanced sound, this cycle might not be my first recommendation for someone looking to own only one set of these works. The expensive Takacs set in particular can be seen as major competition, since they have a similarly balanced approach (without the Viennese flavor) and a similar, slightly superior acoustic. The Emerson Quartet set is now priced similarly to this one and provides a more revolutionary, less traditional approach in a closer, crystal clear acoustic. The Cleveland Quartet are not unlike the Emersons in their virtuosity and recorded acoustic but play with somewhat more sensitivity and flexibility. None of this is to diminish at all the quality of the Alban Berg Quartett's performances, which are distinguished for their intellectual and emotional grasp of the music as well as their superb technical execution. I have found that my opinion of this set improves with time, and upon hearing other recordings. In fact, I might venture at this point to name it as my favorite.

0743233832|5.0|I read a different translation of the [[ASIN:0143036270 Meditations (Penguin Great Ideas)]] a while back.  It is well known that Aurelius' writing style does not always lend itself to ease of reading.  This book cures that ill.  Scholars of the topic will probably say that it makes the work too simple, perhaps.  No matter.  If you want to clearly understand Aurelius' thinking on your first try, this is the book for that task.  The Emperor's views on death, fame and glory, are particularly clear, as he constantly reminds the interloping reader (his works were not meant for publication) that death happens to all, that it is as part of nature as anything else, it is the great equalizer and needs not be feared.  Aurelius also provides good advice on learning how to grow a thick skin by understanding that the slights of others only harm us if we let them, and to expect these slights since how else can we expect boorish or inconsiderate people to behave.  He emphasizes the idea that reason (the mind) need not be affected by what happens to the body, so that pain, illnesses and inconveniences can be borne better and maybe even to advantage.  Christians should not be afraid to read this book if they are well catechized.  Although Aurelius himself persecuted Christians and criticized what he referred to as their hysterics, presumably when they were being tortured or slaughtered in some way, Aurelius' stoicism is consistent with the Christian message of detachment from the things of this world.  Also, Aurelius was a devout Roman who honored the Roman gods.  Christians can simply substitute one God in these thoughts without loss of effect or meaning.  I don't think one can fully appreciate all of Aurelius' teachings alone; a guide or mentor is needed.  My plan is to next read [[ASIN:0674007077 The Inner Citadel: The Meditations of Marcus Aurelius]], a more scholarly treatment.  The Hicks brothers' book should be a good preparation for that.

I consider his bargain to win the love of Gypsy to have been no better than rape. His rage over her suicide was obviously misdirected, but everyone could see he wasn't right in the head. Carmichael's capture felt like Deus Ex Machina, and rather abrupt. It was a bit of a letdown, having that all handled for her. And I wasn't thrilled with Sookie's decision to give him over to Pam. The worst of it was the last confrontation, though, with Newlin, Claude, and the lawyer whose name I just read hours ago and have already forgotten. I admit I had a soft spot for Claude. I felt his interaction with Hunter was a sign of at least some small spark of good in him. I can accept being wrong, and apparently I was. What bothered me was Sookie's lack of feeling regarding his end. This guy was part of her family. He'd shared space in her bed when they'd been lonely, played with Hunter. And yet she felt no sorrow over the way things turned out. Certainly she should be angry at the things he'd done, but her apathy regarding his death really turned me off.<br /><br />I wasn't thrilled with Eric's resolution. I'm not sure what I wanted for him, but his brush off seemed unsatisfactory given the prominence this character has had throughout the series.<br /><br />I will say I was pleased with Pam getting to replace him as Sheriff. For some reason I'd been expecting that to be Bill, but I'm glad it turned out to be Pam. She actually made the most sense, after thinking it through.<br /><br />So, overall, it was an okay, lackluster ending, in my opinion. There were things I liked, but a good amount was disappointing, too.<br /><br />After thinking it over a bit more, there's something else that bothered me about this last book, and perhaps most of the series.  Sookie is never at fault for anything.  Tyrese's vengeance against her is misdirected.  Carmichael's, too.  And Claude is blaming her for things that were his own or Niall's doing.  It's actually one of the major red flags indicative of a Mary Sue when people hate her for reasons that are no fault of her own.  I write some, myself, and it's something I always try to avoid.  Let your characters make mistakes, let them be less than perfect.  Let them, gods forbid, wrong someone.  Let some of their enemies have a legitimate reason to dislike them.  It weakens the story a great deal when the main character can do no wrong.

B00377V6IS|1.0|Since February of 2006, I've had a love-hate relationship with Opeth.  This honestly has annoyed me a lot over the years because at least with run-of-the-mill nu-metal and metalcore sewage like Korn, Killswitch Engage, or Disturbed, I knew immediately to loathe them after some extremely unpleasant exposure, but with Opeth, I've been constantly conflicted on whether or not to like them because they're not they type of band to be outwardly annoying.  The band certainly has tasteful musicianship going for them and the overall sound of their music isn't really all that offensive, but at the same time, their music usually didn't stick with me and usually lacked cohesion.<br /><br />STYLE<br /><br />The style of music in \\"Blackwater Park\\" is best described as progressive metal with strong influences from 70's progressive rock mixed with death metal vocals.  On paper, this sounds pretty ambitious and sounds like an idea that can be executed well.  Unfortunately, the execution of this album is quite lousy, which I'll get to in the topic below.<br /><br />On a sidenote, people need to stop calling Opeth \\"progressive death metal\\" because despite the presence of death metal growls on the music, there aren't many (if any) death metal riffs in their catalog.<br /><br />LACK OF COHESION<br /><br />I say there's a lack of cohesion on this album because with all of the different musical ideas Opeth has on here, there's almost no cohesion among them to have the songs flow smoothly.  Because of this lack of cohesion, listening to it becomes quite a chore.  While listening to the songs, you try to get yourself set up into a certain emotional state according to what you hear, but then the song transitions into something totally different and does so abruptly.  This wouldn't be bad if the band's goal was to create something feeling chaotic (such as Behold the Arctopus, their instrumental compositions are chaotic in all the right ways), but because Opeth tries to be \\"beautiful and poetic\\" much of the time, these transitions from something \\"brutal\\" into something more \\"classy\\" feel really uneven and after a while, you just disconnect yourself from the music and get bored with it.<br /><br />MUSICIANSHIP<br /><br />Mikael Akerfeldt's vocals are a bit of a mixed bag here.  His death metal growls are okay, but they don't even begin to compare to what Martin Van Drunen, Karl Willetts, Alex Camargo, Matt Karki, Ross Dolan, and Jeff Walker can dish out.  His clean vocals seem pretty bland, since I couldn't remember much of any of the clean singing parts that stuck out at me.<br /><br />Mikael's guitar work isn't bad, but like with the music in general, lacks cohesion and focus.  He's capable of making compelling metal riffs when he feels like it, and the solos in many of the songs sound nice, but the disjointed styles in many of the songs make them pretty forgettable.<br /><br />Peter Lindgren handled the guitar as well on this album, and like Akerfeldt, has tasteful skills with his instrument, but other than a few riffs from the more \\"aggressive\\" songs and solos, most of his contributions lack memorability.<br /><br />Martin Mendez's bass guitar, honestly, wasn't all that noticeable in most of the album.  There were some parts of some songs where there's some noticeable basslines, and they sounded nice, but that's all I can say about that.<br /><br />Martin Lopez's drum work, like Mendez's bass, didn't stick out to me all that much, either.  I guess his drum work isn't bad, but given the overall forgettable nature of the music, it's hard for me to draw any specific examples addressing the drums.<br /><br />I know that Steve Wilson of Porcupine Tree fame (I'm not a fan of PT, either) helped produce and provide extra clean vocals on some songs.  The vocals Steve provides on this album were pretty bad.  I think he provided extra vocals in the song \\"Bleak,\\" and they made the song sound even more of a chore to listen to.<br /><br />SONGS<br /><br />Remembering specifics for the songs on this album proved to be as difficult as mastering advanced calculus, so forgive me if there's a lack of detail in describing the songs on here.<br /><br />I think for an album that only has eight songs on here, it's too long for its own good (considering it's eight songs in a 67 minute album).  Now I'm not against long songs, as there's bands like Symphony X, Dream Theater, Burzum, Pan.Thy.Monium, and Phantasmagory that have crafted long songs that go past the 10 minute mark that remain compelling to listen to from beginning to end.  Even Opeth created a superb song called \\"Black Rose Immortal\\" (off the album \\"Morningrise) that's 20 minutes long, and isn't at all boring.  However, on here, many of the longer songs seem really disjointed for me to stay engaged in.<br /><br />If I had to choose any songs that I'd consider decent, I'd say \\"Blackwater Park\\" and \\"The Leper Affinity\\" come close to that.  Despite being two of the longer songs on this album, the \\"metal\\" parts on these songs come closest to what I want to hear in any type of metal album, and with the title track, has probably the best guitar solo in the whole album around the middle of the song.  However, the potential greatness in these two songs is greatly hindered by the \\"soft\\" segments of the songs that don't really fit in with the aggressive parts.<br /><br />\\"Harvest\\" is basically a six minute acoustic piece with clean singing.  The solo in the song sounds pleasing, and the overall feeling of the song is inoffensive, but the song is pretty forgettable at the end of the day.  \\"Patterns in the Ivy\\" is merely a two minute acoustic interlude that functions no more than as filler.<br /><br />I couldn't stand the song \\"Bleak.\\"  Mainly for Steve Wilson's guest vocals mentioned earlier, but while songs like \\"Blackwater Park\\" had better \\"metal\\" segments in the song, the metal parts in \\"Bleak\\" felt pretty lukewarm, so this was a really boring song to listen to.  The other songs like \\"The Drapery Falls\\" and \\"Dirge for November\\" were good examples of the songs that are long and felt like they were going on forever.<br /><br />PRODUCTION<br /><br />The production on this album is very clean, though I think it's more fitting for a progressive rock album than for a progressive metal album.  Then again, Steve Wilson produced the album, so he obvious wanted this to feel more like the former than the latter.<br /><br />The instruments and vocals all come in pretty clearly, except for the bass.  I personally wish the bass was more noticeable than it really was.<br /><br />FINAL WORD<br /><br />\\"Blackwater Park\\" commits one of the worst crimes a metal album can commit, which is being boring.  Most of the time, I was staring at my wristwatch waiting for the album to end.<br /><br />It took me nearly four hours to write this review because the music contained in \\"Blackwater Park\\" is too forgettable to quickly gather my thoughts and write them out in detail.<br /><br />Many people will tell you that this album is a masterpiece in progressive metal or worse, will tell you that this is \\"progressive death metal.\\"  Avoid this and check these albums out instead.<br /><br />Nocturnus:  The Key<br />Atheist:  Unquestionable Presence<br />Coroner:  Punishment for Decadence<br />Phantasmagory:  Odd Sounds<br />Death:  Individual Thought Patterns<br />Cynic:  Focus<br />Gordian Knot:  S/T<br />Behold the Arctopus:  Skullgrid<br />Gorguts:  The Erosion of Sanity<br /><br />Why people go ga-ga over this is beyond me.g specifics for the songs on this album proved to be as difficult as mastering advanced calculus, so forgive me if there's a lack of detail in describing the songs on here.<br /><br />I think for an album that only has eight songs on here, it's too long for its own good (considering it's eight songs in a 67 minute album). 0618001905|5.0|King Leopold's Ghost is the riveting account of one king's quest to turn the Congo into his personal fief. This is non-fiction that reads like fiction, replete with robustly developed heroes and villains and a suspenseful and well-paced narrative arc. And lest one think that this book privileges drama over historical accuracy, the author draws on a vast array of primary source documents in crafting his narrative, the most interesting of which being private journal entries. Shakespeare himself could not have created a character more duplicitous and avaricious than King Leopold. The Belgian monarch is forever solidified in my brain as one of history's worst villains; I knew nothing of his greedy exploits before reading this book. I recommend this without reservation to anyone with even a remote interest in the history of colonialism in Africa. This is absolutely one of the finest works of narrative non-fiction that I have ever read.

It's one of those novels where an elaborate plan is hatched that requires a remarkably unlikely series of events to fall perfectly into place and if even one piece is out of sync the whole plan will collapse in on itself.  But of course, all of these improbable pieces come together perfectly, in completely unconvincing fashion.<br /><br />The far-fetched plot was a problem for me, but one I was prepared to accept, with only occasional eye-rolling, to keep my mind occupied while I was in traffic. One that is perhaps even harder to ignore is the reality that Grisham is simply not a gritty enough author to write convincingly about criminals (unless they're of the white collar variety of course).  It's comical to listen to Grisham's dialogue involving drug dealers, something he manages without the use of a single swear word.  Grisham is better off writing about small town lawyers.  His street hoods and drug dealers sound like characters that belong in an episode of Murder She Wrote.<br /><br />The whole plot is preposterous but I kept listening to it so it must have been entertaining enough I guess.  Grisham is trying to write something clever, but it comes off as silly and mostly predictable. It feels like he isn't trying very hard.  2.5 stars.

B00BK40QO0|5.0|&#34;Gustav Mahler recommends Herr Klemperer as an outstanding musician who despite his youth is already experienced and is predestined for the career of conductor.<br />He vouches for the successful outcome of a probationary appointment and is willing personally to provide further information.&#34;<br /><br />In 1905 in Berlin, twenty year-old Otto Klemperer made his professional debut as a conductor.<br />The work was Mahler's 2nd, the &#34;Resurrection Symphony&#34;.<br />The composer was in the audience.<br />After the concert, Mahler congratulated Klemperer on his performance.<br />Two years later, Mahler wrote a letter of recommendation that secured Klemperer's first professional appointment.<br /><br />Actually, Klemperer only conducted 2% of this Symphony. The other 98% was conducted by Oskar Fried.<br />Fried engaged young Klemperer to conduct the off-stage band in the finale. *<br />You have to start somewhere.<br /><br />Otto Klemperer had a life-long obsession with the music of Mahler, particularly the &#34;Resurrection Symphony&#34;.<br />Between 1950 and 1971, he left us with eight recordings.<br />Amazingly enough, Klemperer holds the record as both the fastest and the slowest conductor of this symphony on record - timings from Peter Fulop's 'Mahler Discography':<br /><br />[hint: for ease of navigation, read the review though to the end, then come back and click on the links.]<br /><br />67:04 = 1950 Sydney Symphony Orchestra broadcast - Fastest performance ever. A harrowing experience: [[ASIN:B00004C4KT Otto Klemperer Discoveries Vol. 1: Mahler: Symphony No. 2 &#34;Resurrection&#34;]]<br /><br />70:51 = 1951 Amsterdam Concertgebouw broadcast with Kathleen Ferrier - Also a harrowing experience, less insane than the Australian performance: [[ASIN:B0000669QZ Mahler: Resurrection Symphony]]<br /><br />75:35 = 1951 Vienna Symphony, studio recording - Still really fast, but a bit more reasonable: [[ASIN:B000001KCH Sym 2/Das Lied Von Der Erde]]<br /><br />79-80 minutes = 1962-65: Four performances on the fast side, but not unreasonably so:<br />Philharmonia (EMI studio recording in this box) + broadcasts by<br />Vienna Philharmonic: [[ASIN:B000001OGX Symphony 2 / Resurrection]] - or - [[ASIN:B0000ARNEF Otto Klemperer Conducts Gustav Mahler]]<br />Philharmonia: [[ASIN:B0006IY5K2 Mahler: Symphony No. 2 'Resurrection'; Mozart: Symphony No. 29]]<br />Bavarian Radio: [[ASIN:B00000AF4T Mahler: Symphony No. 2]]<br /><br />98:50 = 1971 New Philharmonia broadcast - Slowest performance on record - used to be on LP, but has not been issued on CD. The only one I don't own.  Aargh!<br /><br />SCANDAL EXPOSED:<br />Klemperer's EMI recording times in at 80:10. This is actual elapsed time, not the printed time.<br />In 1990, EMI wanted to issue it on one CD, but 80 minute CDs were not possible with the technology of the time, so EMI engineers speeded up the tape.<br />Digital technology makes this possible without altering the pitch.<br />Simple.<br />Just enough for a CD that timed in at 79:18 (thanks to J.M. Hughes for this inside information).<br />This dubious procedure was repeated for the 2000 EMI Great Recordings of the Century release.<br />So this box is the first appearance of Klemperer's EMI Mahler 2nd on CD at the correct speed.<br />As such it is an essential purchase for any serious Mahlerian.<br /><br />The four Klemperer performances from 1962-65 are essentially the same conception.<br />The Bavarian Radio broadcast is marginally more intense than the EMI studio performance.<br />It was also well-recorded in stereo, but the EMI studio recording is unbeatable for clarity and warmth (recorded in Kingsway Hall).<br /><br />Perhaps inspired by the need to remaster the 2nd Symphony, EMI also commissioned new remasterings of Symphonies 4, 7, 9 and Das Lied von der Erde.<br />This is the only volume in the new Klemperer Edition that has had such extensive work.<br />It was worth the effort.<br />I find all transfers preferable in clarity and warmth to previous attempts.<br />One caveat: My ears are 64 years old (ditto the rest of me) and I am probably not the best judge of fine gradations of sound.<br /><br />Klemperer's strings were seated in an arc: First Violins, Basses, Cellos, Violas, Second Violins.<br />Not all violins scrunched together on the left, and lower strings on the right, which you will hear with Bernstein, Karajan and Solti.<br />Their 20th Century seating arrangement has become almost universal, but Klemperer's is the orchestra that Mahler was familiar with.<br />Klemperer's old-fashioned seating plan makes the conductor's job a lot harder.<br />Worth the effort. **<br /><br />Nowadays, thanks to an unfriendly neighbor and a dog who demands three walks a day, I have been doing most of my listening on headphones.<br />The 2nd movement Landler of the 9th Symphony seems to have been composed with headphones in mind.<br />Give it a try (in Klemperer's recording).<br /><br />Mahler's 7th Symphony has never appealed to me.<br />Interestingly, Bruno Walter and Otto Klemperer shared my opinion (they also avoided Symphonies 3, 6 and 8 - I am incredulous).<br />Symphony 7 seems like a let-down after the the raw emotion of Symphony 6.<br />Late in life, Klemperer reconsidered the 7th Symphony and left us with a really weird (meaning SLOW) recording of the work.<br /><br />I still don't get the first four movements, but have come to an appreciation of the 5th movement (CD 4, track 1).<br />This movement has nothing in common with with the rest of the symphony.<br />It seems like a throw-back to the world of the First Symphony.<br />All fanfares, chorales and peasant dances.<br />Its actually quite vulgar, but I now find it invigorating.<br />Klemperer's slow pace actually brings out the vulgarity more than Bernstein's streamlined reading.<br />One curiosity: After the initial drumroll and brass chorale, there is (at 1:05) a theme that sounds for all the world like a Broadway showtune.<br />I only noticed this in Klemperer's recording.<br /><br />My only complaint is the absence of texts and translations for the vocal works.<br />Predictable with these bargain boxes.<br />Still sad.<br /><br />All serious Mahlerians need to own the recordings by Klemperer, Bruno Walter, Oskar Fried and Willem Mengelberg. ***<br />They were part of Mahler's inner circle for the last ten years of his life (he died in 1911).<br /><br />USELESS MAHLER TRIVIA:<br />The new Bach-Haydn box in the EMI Klemperer Edition, [[ASIN:B00BK40QWM Bach, Rameau, Handel, GLuck, Haydn]] includes a Mahler rarity.<br />Christoph Willibald Gluck's Overture to Iphiginie en Aulide (1774) was re-orchestrated by Wagner in 1847.<br />The Gluck-Wagner overture was popular in the first half of the Twentieth Century.<br />Furtwangler, Mengelberg, Richard Strauss and Bruno Walter all conducted it.<br />I believe Klemperer's is the only recording in stereo.<br />According to the booklet notes, Klemperer conducted a version with further revisions by Gustav Mahler.<br />This was done in 1907, when Klemperer was part of Mahler's inner circle.<br />I don't think it's been published.<br />Apparently this is the world premiere recording of the Gluck-Wagner-Mahler Overture to Iphigenie en Aulide.<br />Its a monster.<br /><br />REMASTERING DATES:<br />2012 for Symphonies 2, 4 and 9.<br />2011 for Symphony 7 and Das Lied von der Erde.<br />1999 for five songs with Christa Ludwig (the GROC remastering).<br /><br />*******************************************************************************************************************<br /><br />* In 1924 Oskar Fried recorded Mahler's &#34;Resurrection Symphony&#34; with the Berlin State Opera Orchestra: [[ASIN:B00EIPIL2M Mahler: the Music of Gustav Mahler: Issu]]<br />This was the first ever recording of a Mahler symphony. Its an acoustic recording.<br />A reduced orchestra and chorus were crammed into a room facing a giant horn (no microphones).<br />Nosuch luxury as an off-stage band this time, so Klemperer wasn't needed.<br />Hard to listen to, but worth the effort<br /><br />** Leopold Stokowski is credited with devising the modern seating plan with massed violins on the left.<br />Stokowski liked the richer string sound that resulted when the f-holes of both violin sections were facing the audience.<br />This is not as lewd as it sounds.<br />The f-holes are two f-shaped holes on the top of the violin.<br />They serve to focus and project the sound coming from the interior of the instrument.<br />Violinists seated to the left of the conductor hold their instruments at a 45 degree angle toward the audience.<br />Those to the right hold their instruments at a 45 degree angle away from the audience (unless they're left-handed, then no problem).<br />Sacrificing clarity for fullness of tone: the &#34;Philadelphia Sound.&#34;<br /><br />This new system also made it easier for musicians to stay together, and Twentieth century conductors came increasingly to adopt it<br />(bot not Toscanini or Furtwangler, both of whom passed away before stereo could document their divided violins).<br />Contemporaries of Klemperer who remained loyal to divided violins in the stereo age included Monteux, Boult, Bohm, Kubelik and Bruno Walter.<br />But their recordings never enjoyed the combination of detail and weight of tone that Otto Klemperer's did.<br />Klemperer benefited from the synergy of the Philharmonia Orchestra, producer Walter Legge, the EMI engineering staff, and London's Kingsway Hall.<br />[Fritz Reiner's earliest Chicago Symphony stereo recordings had divided violins, but by 1957 he had given up and adopted massed violins on the left].<br /><br />*** Bruno Walter recorded Symphonies 1, 2, 4, 5, 9 and Das Lied von der Erde in multiple performances for EMI, Decca and Columbia (Sony).<br />Mengelberg recorded the Adagietto of the 5th Symphony in 1926: [[ASIN:B000088DTZ Mengelberg: Wagner; R. Strauss; Mahler]] and a complete broadcast of the 4th Symphony in 1939 (once available on Philips, it can still be found on some smaller labels).<br /><br />P.S. Unforgettable characters are introduced at every turn. The staple characters' situations grow, and become intertwined - all resulting in aesthetic complexity. The lines of good and bad become blurred beyond recognition.  Your love and hate (sometimes both, simultaneously) for the characters will waver almost every episode as you get COMPLETELY blindsided by extremities... it will put on the edge of your seat.<br /><br />If anyone asks me, this is undoubtedly my favorite TV series. I wish I could label myself with something less... contentious... but hey... you can't be satisfied watching Spongebob for the rest of your life, right?

B0083D9MXM|3.0|I'm glad to have this set, mainly as a cultural document.  Stokowski was one of these musicians who caught the public imagination, and he seemed to have a genuine interest in making music accessible to regular folks.  He obviously knew how to get what he wanted from an orchestra, often in resplendent sound.  However, not everything works.  For me \\"the \\"good\\" here are the Mendelssohn and Bizet symphonies, the De Falla suite, the Ives,and the encores.  Music should give pleasure, and these discs give pleasure, even though none of them is \\"the best\\" performance out there.  The questionable, for me are the Bach and Beethoven discs.  Orchestral arrangements of Bach's pieces for solo keyboard instruments sound bloated to me -- not revelatory -- even when the sound is good.  I just can't get into them -- maybe my fault.  Stokowski certainly plays them with conviction.  Also questionable is the \\"Emperor\\" with Gould.  The sound is very good indeed, although everything is a bit \\"up close,\\" and Gould's touch and lucidity and even beauty of tone are a pleasure.  The first movement I liked; with all its little quirks, it was alive, even if a bit fussy.  The second movement didn't work -- the phrasing gets glacial, with each note, it seems, being picked out for inspection.  A sense of song and the forward movement get lost.  The \\"ugly,\\" for me, is the Brahms Second, in sound much inferior to the Beethoven of eleven years earlier, and really lacking a sense of  direction.  In the finale, there's no build up, just extreme dynamic variation, and it all just falls to pieces: a real mess all round.  So . . . I'm not sorry to have the box, but it's mixed bag indeed.  When he was good, he was good . . . when he was bad, he was horrid.

0765365278|4.0|I'm a big Brandon Sanderson fan, and \\"The Way of Kings\\" (Tor, $27.99, 1,008 pages) is a very accomplished book one - and a pretty hefty beginning, as well, checking in at more than 1,000 pages. So I would normally say \\"Jump right in,\\" as it will be a great ride, except for the fact that this may be the first of a ten-book series called the Stormlight Archive.<br /><br />Yes, ten books, and that immediately brings to mind Robert Jordan's seemingly endless Wheel of Time series - which, not coincidentally, Sanderson was hired to complete. And to add even more uncertainty into the equation, Sanderson was supposed to write only one book to wrap up that series (which was begun in 1984), but now will write three.<br /><br />So basically, if you hop on board the Stormlight Archive express, you're on the hook for at least the next decade, assuming that a) it does turn out to be 10 books; b) Sanderson retains his interest in the project; and c) that unlike Jordan, he lives to complete it.<br /><br />Still, Sanderson is a much better writer than Jordan, which is definitely a positive, and \\"The Way of Kings\\" is superior to any book in the Wheel of Time. And as usual, Sanderson's meticulous world-building includes not only an ecology, but a carefully thought-out magical system that has echoes in the culture and society at large.<br /><br />\\"The Way of Kings\\" is set on a planet that is routinely scoured by highstorms, which are powerful, hurricane-like storms that are infused with a magical energy known as Stormlight, or so it appears. The pre-industrial society has Sanderson's usual interesting quirks: In this one, for example, men do not read or write - only women are allowed that skill.<br /><br />The narrative involves the struggle between humans and a humanoid race for control of magical gems that are found in an area known as the Shattered Plains. There are plenty of battles, enhanced with magic, but there are also Sanderson's complex plots and carefully developed characters.<br /><br />I would be unabashedly enthusiastic about \\"The Way of Kings\\" (though it could have used a few more upbeat sections) but for the possibility that it may not finish until we find out who wins the 2022 World Cup. After all, even if all goes as planned, The Wheel of Time will turn to be a 27-year odyssey over 14 volumes.

The corporations let loose the plagues and then generously offer to sell countries the plague-resistant plants - which naturally cannot reproduce so there's plenty of income the next year as well.<br /><br />\\"The Windup Girl\\" is set in steamy Bangkok, where Thailand struggles to maintain its independence from the tyranny of the American agricultural corporations, and Bacigalupi deftly details the political power struggles common to all of human history in a setting of looming threats from disease and the encroaching sea.<br /><br />Bacigalupi is a talented writer, fueled by a passionate point of view, and that combination makes \\"The Windup Girl\\" a powerful, if not exactly uplifting, novel. 0345457692|3.0|I've always been a mystery fan, from Lew Archer to Travis McGee to Hercule Poirot, so mixing a detective story with science fiction doesn't bother me much. But it's only fair to warn prospective readers that `Altered Carbon' (Del Rey, $13.95, 384 pp.) is a lot more hard-boiled detective novel than classical science fiction.<br /><br />Oh, the underpinning is solid scifi: Personalities can be stored electronically and injected into new bodies (called sleeves), and the very rich have cloned sleeves that they can move into on a moment's notice. They even have automatic remote updates so if they're killed, their memories can be restored. The setup involves a Methuselah (one of the rich folks who lives a long time) who commits suicide between one update and the next, and, reincarnated in a new sleeve, wants to know what happened. The police don't like him, or any Meths, as they're called, so they blow him off. He then resleeves (or re-animates, if you will) the hero into a new body on a new planet, and puts him to work solving the crime.<br /><br />Our hero is a nasty ultraviolent sort who's had some special training that makes him extra-tough, which comes in really handy during the many bloody episodes that dot the book. He also has some highly charged encounters with beautiful blondes (some things never change, even in the future) as he unravels one mystery and creates his own elaborate revenge.<br /><br />I confess I was confused by the end, for keeping track of who was in whose body and what had been done to whom was a pretty daunting task, but author Richard K. Morgan had the rhythm and the feel of a Dashiel Hammett novel down pat. If the idea of reading about Sam Spade in a cyberpunk world holds some fascination for you, `Altered Carbon' will be a great ride; if not, though, it won't change your mind about detectives who drink too much and have a taste for snappy repartee while they're getting pistol-whipped.
